  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_414736694" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414736694" />
    <a title="Moon Rose" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose"><img alt="Moon Rose" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597367219p2/5189565.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/414736694?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Moon Rose" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Moon Rose" href="/user/show/5189565-moon-rose">Moon Rose</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/13003304-cancer-ward">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Moon Rose by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5431458-henry-avila">Henry Avila</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=2012-shelf">2012-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=nobel-laureates">nobel-laureates</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5189565-moon-rose?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer414736694" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10829878945166918281">As the <i>cliche</i> goes, <i>money</i> is the root of all evil, and many would agree that indeed it is. On the contrary, it contradicts the essence of what had become human living since time immemorial. As <i>human living</i> immersed itself voluntarily in the deep dark materiality of existence, as it is beleaguered by the sensual pleasures of physicality. In truth, the want of <i>money</i> is only a direct object. It appears only as the end goal to attain the inexhaustible, human yearning for <i>
  <b>material happiness</b>
</i>. This</span>
  <span id="freeText10829878945166918281" style="display:none">As the <i>cliche</i> goes, <i>money</i> is the root of all evil, and many would agree that indeed it is. On the contrary, it contradicts the essence of what had become human living since time immemorial. As <i>human living</i> immersed itself voluntarily in the deep dark materiality of existence, as it is beleaguered by the sensual pleasures of physicality. In truth, the want of <i>money</i> is only a direct object. It appears only as the end goal to attain the inexhaustible, human yearning for <i>
  <b>material happiness</b>
</i>. This burning desire for earthly comforts and the luxurious provisions that it usually comes with that provides the ability to take <i>control</i> and get ahead, can be define as the true cause or rather, the <i>mother root</i> of <i>ALL EVIL</i>. As <i>
  <b>greed</b>
</i>, or the <i>
  <b>love of power</b>
</i> is blurred invisibly deep within the human consciousness, enabling the flesh to do harm against another as it slowly infects the soul like a <i>carcinogen</i> to decay and rot, losing its full capacity to <i>love</i>.<br /><br /><i>Cancer Ward</i> is an aftermath of this terrible human sin, as it is set against the Post Stalin era in Soviet Russia. This is a social condition of loose morals foreseen by <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a>
</i>, as it is prophetically depicted in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5695.Demons" title="Demons by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Demons</a>
</i>, in which <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/128382.Leo_Tolstoy" rel="nofollow noopener">Leo Tolstoy</a>
</i> tries to redeem through his Christ like vision of passivity.<br /><br />Soviet Russia under the tyrannical regime of Stalin was gripped by the might of his human cruelty, social oppression and the injustice of his political repression, casting his dark shadow over the country in the midst of turmoil, as it blanketed the tortured lives of the many into the oblivion of dreamless isolation.<br /><br /><i>Cancer Ward</i> is a seamless reenactment of this fatal social condition, surprisingly overwhelming in the pureness of its simplicity, as it is written in the fluidity of <i>Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</i>'s masterful technique. It is his symbolic representation of an ailing society on the brink of death, as the Russian people hope against hope and tried to hold on to the flickering light of uncertainty after being plunged into the abyss of <i>moral degradation</i> and exposed into the ensuing darkness of <i>spiritual deterioration</i>.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>THE SOULS IN THE WARD: Blind Men Walking on the Shadows of the Living Dead</u>
</b><br /><blockquote>
  <i>There are people with eyes who choose to be blind, and there are people who breathe, but consider themselves dead.</i>
</blockquote>Like a true virtuoso <i>Solzhenitsyn</i> paints with so much dexterity, using only the easel of his narrative technique, the lifeless existence in a <i>gulag</i> in <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17125.One_Day_in_the_Life_of_Ivan_Denisovich" title="One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich by Aleksandr I. Solzhenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</a>
</i>, capturing in utter lucidity a day in the life of an imprisoned man forced into a labor camp. In <i>Cancer Ward</i>, he put these so called <i>imprisoned men</i>, both literally and figuratively vis-a-vis with the people, whose sheer blindness contributed to even greater extent to their perpetual ordeal. <i>Solzhenitsyn</i> puts all of them in the same footing, using cancer as God's Sacred equalizer, and death as a Divine declaration of equality...<br /><br /><b>The Blind Fanatics: The Scheming Official and The Quiet Geologist</b><br /><br />The underlying vileness in the character of <i>
  <b>Pavel Nikolayevich Rusanov</b>
</i> is a representation of the extent of Stalin's deadly tentacles. The perfect epitome of blind obedience mingled with a stringent conviction in an inane and senseless belief. This guiltless and scheming official is behind the deportation, torture and imprisonment of innocent people, believing that what he does is only a testament of loyalty to his country, but in reality, he is just a common criminal who abuses his power out of spite. <i>Rusanov</i> is the <i>antithesis</i> to the character of <i>
  <b>Oleg Filimonovich Kostoglotov</b>
</i>, <i>Solzhenitsyn</i>'s main protagonist, who perhaps mostly resembles him in character and belief, as the different ideologies of these two men lie in the opposite side of the spectrum.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>Vadim</b>
</i>, on the other hand, is the silent geologist obsessed to leave a legacy in the world, believing in full heart that it will engraved his immortality in the minds of the people. As he realizes the imminent danger his into, his idea is to make the most of his short and brief life, exhausting all his energy, all that remained in the fulfillment of his earthly dream of recognition. His is what could be referred as the <i>Divine blindness of being</i>. A self devoid of any form of spirituality that loses one's connection to his own eternal soul. It is a kind of blinded existence common in all humanity that embraces the ephemeral part of life, holding on to the fleeting and temporal aspects of happiness.<br /><br /><b>Blind No More: Conquering Fear on the Brink of Death</b><br /><br />Death can be regarded as the Divine transition to immortality. It is the sheer moment of detachment, as the spirit leaves its temporal vessel, it surrenders to the <i>Source of Light</i> back to its eternal rest. It is the moment of <i>Enlightenment</i> that usually begins with most men when they are on the brink of death.<br /><br />The tall, lanky figure of <i>
  <b>Aleksei Filippovich Shulubin</b>
</i> is on the verge of this precipice. He is perhaps the most identifiable among the patients in the ward. The irritating glacial stare of his popping eyes that emanates from his blank timid nature hides the terrible guilt inside of him. It is the guilt of betrayal, of surrendering to <i>fear</i> and succumbing to a belief he truly opposes in order to preserve his life and protect his family.<br /><br />With his tortured mind full of guilt and a slowly dying body, he breaks his silence, as he lets out his evolved <i>being</i> with a sudden outburst during a heated discord in defense of <i>Kostoglotov</i>. It particularly highlights his character, making him at par with the rest of the patients in terms of spiritual maturity. His discourse on the significance of <i>
  <b>ethical socialism</b>
</i> shows the depth of his thought, shining with a penetrating illumination of an <i>enlightened</i> soul.<br /><br />This is no different from <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Dostoevsky</a>
</i>'s approach. To reach the level of <i>Shulubin</i>'s understanding is to bridge the gap between suffering and happiness, enabling the spirit to arise amidst turmoil, as it dissipates <i>fear</i> and unleashes the <i>lightness</i> of the <i>true being</i>.<br /><br /><b>The Converted: Seeing the Illumination of Life at the Point of Death</b><br /><br />For most men, <i>life</i> is to be lived to the fullest. It is like living on the pretense of no tomorrow, exhausting the earth's resources for selfish reasons. This is a blind notion of existence clouded by the dark shadow of <i>
  <b>materialism</b>
</i>. It hinders the slightest trace of mortality in a human life, believing that the eternal glory lies in the fulfillment of one's OWN transient satisfaction.<br /><br />It is living life with a full stomach, <i>leaving</i> behind an empty and <i>starving</i> soul...<br /><br />The filth and rot of <i>
  <b>Yefrem Podduyev</b>
</i>'s hungry soul responded to the erudite words of the <i>Tolstoyan</i> thought of <i>love</i> while reading one of his books. It is <i>his</i> starving soul trapped inside his dying body on the verge of extinguishing its immortality that attempts to communicate with the Divine Words, as it seeks its own salvation.<br /><br />Like <i>Vadim</i>, <i>Yefrem</i> enclosed his soul into an apocryphal belief throughout his existence. It is an indifferent life devoid of any compassion, yet before he reaches his end. He still manages to feed his spirit, fighting against the whim of his physical body with an illumination worthy to salvage his past wrong doings, as it rectifies and eventually frees a life into the transformative power of <i>love</i>.<br /><br /><i>Solzhenitsyn</i> did not explore much into <i>Yefrem</i>'s life. He only gives us a glimpse of an <i>equanimity</i> against the roaring turbulence of life at the point of death.<br /><br /><u>
  <b>THE RESURRECTION OF THE LIVING DEAD: The Ex Prisoner in Perpetual Exile Amidst the Social Cancer</b>
</u><br /><br />There is a disease more fatal than <i>
  <b>Cancer</b>
</i>. It gnaws not the skin, but the spirit within. It cripples not only an individual, but the condition of the collective whole. Its destructive elements damage not only the body, but the destiny of the human race, polluting men into moral corruption with the complete lack of compassion.<br /><br /><i>Solzhenitsyn</i> refers to this <i>
  <b>social cancer</b>
</i> in this pragmatic novel, ironically making it appear side by side with the actual disease itself, as he mingles them together in the eloquence of his style. The character of <i>
  <b>Oleg Filimonovich Kostoglotov</b>
</i> is the best personification of this symbolic fusion, as he seems to appear as the extension, or rather a sequel to the life and times of <i>Ivan Denisovich</i> after the <i>Gulag</i>, reflecting the millions of innocent Russian lives shattered by the intense grip of this social disease and at the same time, suffering from the fatal malady itself that blows his already wasted life to further uncertainty.<br /><br />It is also through <i>Kostoglotov</i> that <i>Solzhenitsyn</i> shows the debilitating effects of a force deliberately stricken upon another's life, as it squanders the promise of his youth, alienating him from society as it closes out all possibilities in life and destroying even one's capacity to hope, perhaps a mere reflection of <i>Solzhenitsyn</i>'s own experiences and perceptions of Soviet Russia after his exile. <br /><br />This is evident in <i>Cancer Ward</i>'s open ending that seems to connote one's own hopelessness, as if submitting helplessly to the irrevocable hands of destiny for comfort and answers, represented by <i>Kostoglotov</i>'s feeling of detachment, alienation and indecisiveness, leaving all to fate to put back the broken pieces of his life.<br /><br />Only Time can tell when human sins will be absolved in the true sense of forgiveness, enabling humans to reach the pinnacle of spiritual maturity that allows everyone to understand in perfect harmony thereby <i>
  <b>resurrecting</b>
</i> the souls for the absolute <i>
  <b>redemption</b>
</i> of all. ☾</span>
  <a data-text-id="10829878945166918281" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2732872471" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2732872471" />
    <a title="John" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21938800-john"><img alt="John" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1373055112p2/21938800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2732872471?book_show_action=true">Mar 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John" href="/user/show/21938800-john">John</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/1036512.Cancer_Ward">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21938800-john?shelf=russian-authors">russian-authors</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2732872471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9037935631617654985">Solzhenitsyn deservedly earned the Nobel Prize for this and his other works in 1970. The story is about a small group of patients receiving treatment in a cancer ward in one of the old Soviet Union Central Asian republics. <br/><br/>Oleg Kostoglotov is a wonderful character as are Rusanov, Dyoma, Yefrem, Vega, Zoya and of course Shulubin a Bolshevik scholar regretting all the comprises made to survive. The story is also semi-autobiographical and draws on Solzhenitsyn’s own gulags and cancer experiences fo</span>
  <span id="freeText9037935631617654985" style="display:none">Solzhenitsyn deservedly earned the Nobel Prize for this and his other works in 1970. The story is about a small group of patients receiving treatment in a cancer ward in one of the old Soviet Union Central Asian republics. <br /><br />Oleg Kostoglotov is a wonderful character as are Rusanov, Dyoma, Yefrem, Vega, Zoya and of course Shulubin a Bolshevik scholar regretting all the comprises made to survive. The story is also semi-autobiographical and draws on Solzhenitsyn’s own gulags and cancer experiences for this masterpiece. The doctors, patients and the sense of desperation and acceptance is captured with the radiation, surgical and quack treatments <br /><br />Amazingly the translation is excellent and it’s a shame the translators name is not mentioned in my edition. Life in the cancer ward is like a microcosm of the Soviet Union around the time that Stalin recently died and a wave of change for exiles was occurring. Many in the small group worry about their roles in the great purges and the possible implications. The visit to the zoo and parallels of people losing their freedom is evident. Perhaps the most poignant statement for me was ‘An evil man threw tobacco in the macaque-rhesus’s eyes.’ A reference to Stalinism and the people being blinded by their own fears of being sent to gulags other punished by the regime.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9037935631617654985" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2194777967775674267"><i>Cancer Ward</i> is like all the other greats of Russian literature: Dense, passionate and rewarding. This truly beautiful novel is, to me, the best Russian novel of the twentieth century, and Solzhenitsyn is one of Russia's greatest writers ever to have lived.</span>

<div id="review_212133454" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/212133454" />
    <a title="Bethan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1040996-bethan"><img alt="Bethan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1475468854p2/1040996.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/212133454?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bethan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bethan" href="/user/show/1040996-bethan">Bethan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040996-bethan?shelf=regular-fiction">regular-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1040996-bethan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer212133454" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2159919678552523872">The greyness of the cancer theme (it's set in a cancer ward) is just like a mirroring backdrop for the Soviet Union that it showcases. Vignettes of the lives of patients, doctors, nurses and others; from the high-ranking and staunch Soviet bureaucrat Rusanov to the poor exile Kostoglotov, it breaks their political and ideological positions down to their narrower human concerns and desires, such as the materialism of Rusanov's home that he enjoys, or Kostoglotov's desire for a woman that takes up</span>
  <span id="freeText2159919678552523872" style="display:none">The greyness of the cancer theme (it's set in a cancer ward) is just like a mirroring backdrop for the Soviet Union that it showcases. Vignettes of the lives of patients, doctors, nurses and others; from the high-ranking and staunch Soviet bureaucrat Rusanov to the poor exile Kostoglotov, it breaks their political and ideological positions down to their narrower human concerns and desires, such as the materialism of Rusanov's home that he enjoys, or Kostoglotov's desire for a woman that takes up much of the novel. This is a very humanistic novel and it shows things like one of the overworked doctors getting cancer herself and how her colleagues react, or of the little fights the patients have with the doctors to be told what is going on with them medically, or of what it is like on the trains when Kostoglotov goes out. Life is showcased and you can't ask for much more than that. <br /><br />------------<br /><br />I enjoyed the argument that Rusanov's golden daughter had, about "insincerity" in literature, with a patient on the ward - against realism; that literature should be about ideals of the future. From her, it sounded like that literature should be propaganda, but this debate also sounded curiously archaic - I am reminded that people used to have debates on what literature ought to be while these days there seems to be tacit acceptance that literature can be various things. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2159919678552523872" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_423143780" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/423143780" />
    <a title="Marc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2876105-marc"><img alt="Marc" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1569160429p2/2876105.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/423143780?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marc" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marc" href="/user/show/2876105-marc">Marc</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=communism">communism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=soviet-union">soviet-union</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2876105-marc?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer423143780" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16987441198217196787">In this novel Solsjenitsyn is above all a Russian writer: lots of characters (patients, doctors, nurses in the cancer ward of a hospital, somewhere in Central Asia, in the mid-50's, in full Soviet era). He takes his time to describe some of these characters in full, and through them he brings up existential, political and social questions. Let's say he offers a mix of Tolstoi and Dostojevski, although he is less whirling and feverish than those two classic models.<br/>The construction of the novel is</span>
  <span id="freeText16987441198217196787" style="display:none">In this novel Solsjenitsyn is above all a Russian writer: lots of characters (patients, doctors, nurses in the cancer ward of a hospital, somewhere in Central Asia, in the mid-50's, in full Soviet era). He takes his time to describe some of these characters in full, and through them he brings up existential, political and social questions. Let's say he offers a mix of Tolstoi and Dostojevski, although he is less whirling and feverish than those two classic models.<br />The construction of the novel is a bit awkward: some storylines are unfinished, and sometimes I had the impression Solsjenitsyn had lost course. In the end there's only one character left, the others fade away.<br />But these shortcomings are compensated for by the political and humanistic message. Solsjenitsyn gives a most uncompromising critic on the soviet system, namely by showing the effect it has on real people. The cancer is kind of a metaphore for the totalitarian system. But the strongest storyline is that of people fighting the horrible disease, coping with the possibility of death and the question what they have made of their life. <br />The last 100 pages are beautiful: the main character, Kostoglotov, leaves the cancer ward, still a bit unstable and enters the nearby city, observing everything with wondering eyes; it has been 12 years since he has been in the real world. His voyage has ups and downs, with hope and disappointment, and in the end accepting his fate. Rightout beautiful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16987441198217196787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131176268?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780394604992" href="/book/show/592195.Cancer_Ward">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2095414-john?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2095414-john?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131176268" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12691000939185854062">I was slow to pick this one up, "After all," I thought, "how interesting can a book be about a ward full of cancer patients?" The answer is very interesting. This is an excellent book. It is a remarkable contrast to the epic fictional works of his that I've read of his. It is intimate, romantic, personal, and tragic. I heartily recommend this book. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_131850615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/131850615" />
    <a title="Larry Bassett" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett"><img alt="Larry Bassett" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1390874855p2/3816725.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131850615?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry Bassett" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry Bassett" href="/user/show/3816725-larry-bassett">Larry Bassett</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 1199066877" href="/book/show/674730.Cancer_Ward">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3816725-larry-bassett?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131850615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1451378386888917591"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/254316.Cancer_Ward' title='Cancer Ward by Aleksandr I. Solzhenitsyn' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cancer Ward</a> sat on my parents’ bookshelf for most of my adult life. I never was tempted to touch it or any of the other Solzhenitsyn books. I was really terrified by those Russian authors. You know the ones: Pushkin, Dostoyevsky, Pasternak, Nabokov. Some of those books were big! And I was not a big book person.<br/> <br/><i>Cancer Ward</i> forced me to reassess my fear. Now <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70561.The_Gulag_Archipelago_1918_1956' title='The Gulag Archipelago 1918-1956 by Aleksandr I. Solzhenitsyn' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Gulag Archipelago</a> is on my to-read shelf with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7144.Crime_and_Punishment_' title='Crime and Punishment  by Fyodor Dostoyevsky' rel='nofollow noopener'>Crime and Punishment</a> and more of those Russian novels to come one of these days!<br/> <br/>This book</span>
  <span id="freeText1451378386888917591" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/254316.Cancer_Ward" title="Cancer Ward by Aleksandr I. Solzhenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">Cancer Ward</a> sat on my parents’ bookshelf for most of my adult life. I never was tempted to touch it or any of the other Solzhenitsyn books. I was really terrified by those Russian authors. You know the ones: Pushkin, Dostoyevsky, Pasternak, Nabokov. Some of those books were big! And I was not a big book person.<br /> <br /><i>Cancer Ward</i> forced me to reassess my fear. Now <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/70561.The_Gulag_Archipelago_1918_1956" title="The Gulag Archipelago 1918-1956 by Aleksandr I. Solzhenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">The Gulag Archipelago</a> is on my to-read shelf with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7144.Crime_and_Punishment_" title="Crime and Punishment  by Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Crime and Punishment</a> and more of those Russian novels to come one of these days!<br /> <br />This book is about a cancer ward in Russia in the 1950s. But it is really about people overcoming or succumbing to adversity in life. There is plenty of adversity and denial in a cancer ward. The book examines the people on a male cancer ward: their relationships with each other and with the medical staff. <br /><br />I am interested in going on rounds with the doctors listening to what they say to the dying patients about their improving conditions. But, of course, what got the book banned in the Soviet Union in 1968 was that allegorically the patients were the country with its oppression of the people. <br /><br /><blockquote>The novel makes many allegorical references to the state of Soviet Russia, in particular the quote from Kostoglotov: "A man dies from a tumour, so how can a country survive with growths like labour camps and exiles?" Source: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cancer_Ward">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cancer_Ward</a> </blockquote><br /><br />And there is the love story. Patient falls in love with doctor, the doctor feels the same but they never deal with their feelings directly. The patient leaves the cancer ward and returns voluntarily to the city of his exile, declining to pursue his love of the doctor, fearing that his cancer treatment has left him impotent.<br /> <br />It seemed like it took me forever to read this 536 page book. My enjoyment level was up and down. I was reading this book with a couple of other books and the other books kept winning out. Like so many of the books I am reading these days, <i>Cancer Ward</i> lost my interest in several places but then picked up again just when I was about to give up. Maybe the book has less relevance because the oppressive days of exile in Soviet Russia that were the target of the book seem a part of the distant past. While the politics of the book that caused such serious problems for Solzhenitsyn are part of the past, the human and medical story behind the allegory still seems relevant and even gripping to me. <br /><br />Once banned in Soviet Russia, the book is buried by the many years since its publication. But I am glad I took it off my parents’ bookshelf and brought it home to read. I give it three stars because I did lose interest occasionally but I found many four star sections where the story has outlived the politics of the era. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1451378386888917591" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_435041142" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/435041142" />
    <a title="Jenni" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10035303-jenni"><img alt="Jenni" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1462894497p2/10035303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/435041142?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenni" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenni" href="/user/show/10035303-jenni">Jenni</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10035303-jenni?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10035303-jenni?shelf=classic-literature">classic-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer435041142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2326464024340980007">There's something sobering about this novel.<br/><br/>Weighing in at over 500 pages and easily the heaviest thing in my bag, <i>Cancer Ward</i> would seem to come to a definite conclusion, be it comforting or disturbing, by its denouement. But Solzhenitsyn offers nothing of the sort. Rather, we must revel in the beautiful ambiguity of this novel, and, in so doing, revel in the often frustrating, poignant, and somber ambiguity of life. <br/><br/>This novel is at once both a metaphorical critique of Soviet Russia as well as</span>
  <span id="freeText2326464024340980007" style="display:none">There's something sobering about this novel.<br /><br />Weighing in at over 500 pages and easily the heaviest thing in my bag, <i>Cancer Ward</i> would seem to come to a definite conclusion, be it comforting or disturbing, by its denouement. But Solzhenitsyn offers nothing of the sort. Rather, we must revel in the beautiful ambiguity of this novel, and, in so doing, revel in the often frustrating, poignant, and somber ambiguity of life. <br /><br />This novel is at once both a metaphorical critique of Soviet Russia as well as a touching story of numerous multi-faceted characters. From almost humorously heartless Rusanov, to young and lovable Dyomka, to our faithful protaganist Kostoglotov, there isn't much of humanity that Solzhenitsyn doesn't touch upon with his piercing thought. And touch he does: Solzhenitsyn set the novel in a Cancer Ward on the outskirts of the USSR in the mid-50's, and used this locale masterfully as a touching-point for his clear observations of both life and death. <br /><br />Some men survive to see better times; many fates are left unknown; and, unavoidably, with a sick punch to my gut, a few men suffer throughout, never to live their lives with health and zeal again. <br /><br />At points, the feel of decay in the Ward is tangible. Tomb-like, almost. As though there is nowhere for these varied men to go, nothing for them to hope for, their robust arms, stomachs, legs all wasting away to nothingness. <br /><br />And yet, <b>there is life to live</b>. Kostoglotov realizes this and fights for his freedom with all that he has; he sneaks books, questions doctors, does all that a peasant man can do to try to take hold of his life once again. He struggles with the decision of whether or not to take a hormone treatment that will give him the gift of freedom for a few years--but at the expense of his virility. And he wonders: what is the price of a life? At what point do you cut the cord? Is freedom truly freedom if you cannot follow your passions? <br /><br />Amidst these back-and-forth daily concerns is the overarching concern of Soviet society. The Cancer Ward is a microcosm for the USSR at large; it holds both party leaders and party exiles, camp guards and camp prisoners in its fleshy grip. <b>Their cancers bring them down to the same level: human. And it is terrifying.</b> Kostoglotov often considers the senseless cruelty that comes with life.... of cancer, of the Soviet party leaders, of average, normal human beings. He thinks of the monkey at the zoo that has been blinded by a man who threw tobacco at its eyes. And he learns that there will never be a respite from senseless, reasonless malice; it is the sober state of human nature that a single man can, at will, unwind all of your life and its promises and treasures. <br /><br />Just like that.... </span>
  <a data-text-id="2326464024340980007" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('QHrLB75h7laOn0sEPP6wcW88KVVUvWqA0oBa5K4E6px6XKLVBT8nPim0/2XLC7quuWPOnZow5OeJ/hxVY0cYBg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

The 1st three volumes were written from 1925 to \'32 &amp; published in the Soviet magazine October in 1928–32. The 4th volume was finished in 1940. The English translation of the 1st three volumes appeared under this title in 1934. The novel is considered one of the most significant works of Russian literature in the 20th century. It depicts the lives &amp; struggles of Don Cossacks during WWI, the Russian Revolution &amp; Russian Civil War. In 1965, Sholokhov was awarded the Nobel Prize for Literature for this novel. The authorship of the novel is contested by some literary critics &amp; historians, who believe it wasn\'t entirely written by Sholokhov.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"17656636212969698242\" href=\"#\" The novel\'s vision of Soviet life in the 1930s is so ferociously accurate that it could not be published during its author\'s lifetime and appeared only in a censored edition in the 1960s. Its truths are so enduring that its language has become part of the common Russian speech.\n\nOne hot spring, the devil arrives in Moscow, accompanied by a retinue that includes a beautiful naked witch and an immense talking black cat with a fondness for chess and vodka. The visitors quickly wreak havoc in a city that refuses to believe in either God or Satan. But they also bring peace to two unhappy Muscovites: one is the Master, a writer pilloried for daring to write a novel about Christ and Pontius Pilate; the other is Margarita, who loves the Master so deeply that she is willing literally to go to hell for him. What ensues is a novel of inexhaustible energy, humor, and philosophical depth, a work whose nuances emerge for the first time in Diana Burgin\'s and Katherine Tiernan O\'Connor\'s splendid English       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/19771050.Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn">About Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11372781156551728584"><b>Aleksandr Isayevich Solzhenitsyn</b> (Russian: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13622681._____________________________' title='Александр Иса́евич Солженицын' rel='nofollow noopener'>Александр Иса́евич Солженицын</a>) was a Soviet and Russian novelist, dramatist, and historian. Through his writings he helped to make the world aware of the Gulag, the Soviet Union's forced labor camp system—particularly <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> and <i>One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</i>, two of his best-known works. <br/><br/>Solzhenitsyn was awarded the Nobel Prize in </span>
  <span id="freeText11372781156551728584" style="display:none"><b>Aleksandr Isayevich Solzhenitsyn</b> (Russian: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13622681._____________________________" title="Александр Иса́евич Солженицын" rel="nofollow noopener">Александр Иса́евич Солженицын</a>) was a Soviet and Russian novelist, dramatist, and historian. Through his writings he helped to make the world aware of the Gulag, the Soviet Union's forced labor camp system—particularly <i>The Gulag Archipelago</i> and <i>One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</i>, two of his best-known works. <br /><br />Solzhenitsyn was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1970. He was exiled from the Soviet Union in 1974 and returned to Russia in 1994. Solzhenitsyn was the father of Ignat Solzhenitsyn, a conductor and pianist.<br /><br />AKA<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16981844.Alexander_Solzenitsyn" title="Alexander Solzenitsyn" rel="nofollow noopener">Alexander Solzenitsyn</a> (English, alternate)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/17891441.____________________" title="Αλεξάντρ Σολζενίτσιν" rel="nofollow noopener">Αλεξάντρ Σολζενίτσιν</a> (Greek)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11372781156551728584" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
